  > Brutal Production Reality
  > 
  > Most enterprise AI failures are not technical.
  > 
  > They are:
  > 
  > Failure	Root Cause
  > Incident not resolved	No owner
  > Security issue ignored	Wrong owner
  > Compliance issue missed	No compliance owner
  > Agent abandoned	Ownership drift
  > Slow recovery	No escalation
  > Bad deployment	No release manager
  > Audit failure	No accountable person
  > Enterprise AgentOps Maturity (26 Tables)
  > 
  > You now have:
  > 
  > Core Registry Layer
  > -------------------
  > agent_registry
  > skill_registry
  > tool_registry
  > mcp_server_registry
  > 
  > Relationship Layer
  > ------------------
  > agent_skill_mapping
  > agent_tool_mapping
  > agent_mcp_mapping
  > 
  > Execution Layer
  > ---------------
  > agent_flow
  > agent_invocation
  > agent_memory
  > 
  > Quality Layer
  > -------------
  > agent_scorecard
  > agent_evaluation
  > agent_feedback
  > 
  > Governance Layer
  > ----------------
  > approval_request
  > agent_guardrail
  > agent_security
  > agent_policy
  > 
  > Operations Layer
  > ----------------
  > agent_health
  > agent_state
  > agent_trace
  > agent_incident
  > agent_cost
  > 
  > Platform Layer
  > --------------
  > agent_version
  > agent_router
  > agent_dependency
  > agent_team
  > 
  > Next logical table:
  > 
  > 27. agent_sla
  > 
  > Because after ownership, the next enterprise question is:
  > 
  > What service level is this agent expected to deliver?
  > 
  > Availability?
  > Latency?
  > Accuracy?
  > Resolution Time?
  > Cost?
  > Recovery Time?27. agent_sla — Service Level Agreement / Reliability Table
  > 
  > This table answers:
  > 
  > What is the expected service level of this agent?
  > How fast should it respond?
  > How accurate should it be?
  > How quickly must incidents be resolved?
  > 
  > Without SLA:
  > 
  > Agent is running
  > 
  > But nobody knows:
  > 
  > Is it meeting expectations?
  > Why This Table Exists
  > 
  > Business asks:
  > 
  > Can I trust this agent for production?
  > 
  > Management asks:
  > 
  > What happens if it fails?
  > 
  > Operations asks:
  > 
  > How fast must we recover?
  > 
  > SLA answers all three.
  > 
  > Purpose
  > Area	Purpose
  > Main Purpose	Define measurable service commitments
  > Business	Set expectations
  > Operations	Measure reliability
  > Governance	Define accountability
  > Incident Management	Recovery targets
  > Vendor Management	Compare performance
  > Relationship
  > agent_registry
  >       ↓
  > agent_sla
  >       ↓
  > agent_health
  >       ↓
  > agent_incident
  >       ↓
  > agent_scorecard
  > SQL Table
  > CREATE TABLE agent_sla (
  > 
  >     sla_id VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
  > 
  >     agent_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     sla_name VARCHAR(200),
  > 
  >     availability_target DECIMAL(5,2),
  > 
  >     latency_target_ms INT,
  > 
  >     accuracy_target DECIMAL(5,2),
  > 
  >     success_rate_target DECIMAL(5,2),
  > 
  >     mttr_target_minutes INT,
  > 
  >     mtta_target_minutes INT,
  > 
  >     max_cost_per_run DECIMAL(10,4),
  > 
  >     max_incidents_per_month INT,
  > 
  >     support_model VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     escalation_policy VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     status VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     owner_team VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     created_at TIMESTAMP
  > );
  > SLA Categories
  > SLA Type	Example
  > Availability SLA	99.9% uptime
  > Accuracy SLA	95% accuracy
  > Latency SLA	< 5 sec
  > Cost SLA	<$0.10/run
  > Incident SLA	MTTR < 2 hr
  > Approval SLA	<30 min
  > Security SLA	Critical response <15 min
  > Compliance SLA	Audit evidence <24 hr
  > Example SLAs
  > Claims Agent
  > Metric	Target
  > Availability	99.95%
  > Accuracy	95%
  > Latency	<4 sec
  > Cost	<$0.05/run
  > MTTR	<2 hr
  > Fraud Agent
  > Metric	Target
  > Availability	99.99%
  > Accuracy	98%
  > Latency	<2 sec
  > MTTR	<30 min
  > Incident Agent
  > Metric	Target
  > Availability	99.99%
  > MTTA	<5 min
  > MTTR	<15 min
  > Accuracy	95%
  > Key SLA Metrics
  > Availability
  > 
  > Formula:
  > 
  > Availability=
  > Total Time
  > Total Time−Downtime
  > 	​
  > 
  > ×100
  > 
  > Target:
  > 
  > 99.9%+
  > Success Rate
  > 
  > Success Rate=
  > Total Runs
  > Successful Runs
  > 	​
  > 
  > ×100
  > 
  > MTTR
  > Mean Time To Recovery
  > 
  > Example:
  > 
  > Incident Opened: 10:00
  > Resolved: 10:20
  > 
  > MTTR = 20 minutes
  > MTTA
  > Mean Time To Acknowledge
  > 
  > Example:
  > 
  > Incident Opened: 10:00
  > Acknowledged: 10:03
  > 
  > MTTA = 3 minutes
  > Runtime Example
  > 
  > Incident Agent:
  > 
  > Availability = 99.95%
  > Latency = 2.1 sec
  > Accuracy = 94%
  > 
  > SLA:
  > 
  > Availability = PASS
  > Latency = PASS
  > Accuracy = FAIL
  > 
  > Creates:
  > 
  > SLA Breach Alert
  > SLA States
  > State	Meaning
  > Green	Meeting SLA
  > Yellow	Near breach
  > Red	Breached
  > Critical	Major breach
  > Waived	Approved exception
  > How It Works
  > Agent Executes
  >       ↓
  > Collect Metrics
  >       ↓
  > Compare Against SLA
  >       ↓
  > Breach?
  >  ┌────┴────┐
  >  No        Yes
  >  ↓         ↓
  > Pass    Create Alert
  >             ↓
  >        Notify Owner
  >             ↓
  >        Create Incident
  > Production Issues
  > 1. Unrealistic SLA
  > 
  > Example:
  > 
  > 99.999%
  > 
  > for a non-critical HR chatbot.
  > 
  > Result:
  > 
  > Constant violations
  > 2. Missing SLA
  > 
  > Agent exists.
  > 
  > No target defined.
  > 
  > Result:
  > 
  > Cannot measure success
  > 3. Wrong SLA
  > 
  > Claims Agent:
  > 
  > Accuracy Target = 70%
  > 
  > Too low.
  > 
  > 4. SLA Drift
  > 
  > Business changes.
  > 
  > SLA not updated.
  > 
  > 5. Dependency SLA Mismatch
  > 
  > Agent:
  > 
  > 99.99%
  > 
  > Dependency:
  > 
  > 95%
  > 
  > Impossible to achieve.
  > 
  > Mitigation
  > Risk	Solution
  > Unrealistic SLA	Tier-based SLA
  > Missing SLA	Mandatory onboarding
  > Drift	Quarterly review
  > Dependency mismatch	Dependency analysis
  > Frequent breaches	Root cause review
  > Poor metrics	Standard KPI catalog
  > SLA Monitoring Dashboard
  > Metric	Target
  > Availability	99.95%
  > Accuracy	95%
  > Success Rate	98%
  > MTTA	<5 min
  > MTTR	<30 min
  > Cost	<$0.05
  > SLA Breaches	0
  > Open Breaches	Track
  > Supervisor Agent Checks
  > 
  > Before Execution:
  > 
  > Is agent meeting SLA?
  > 
  > After Execution:
  > 
  > Did latency breach SLA?
  > 
  > After Incident:
  > 
  > Did MTTR exceed target?
  > 
  > During Release:
  > 
  > Will new version impact SLA?
  > SLA Tiers
  > Tier 1 — Mission Critical
  > 
  > Examples:
  > 
  > Fraud Agent
  > Security Agent
  > Incident Agent
  > 
  > Targets:
  > 
  > 99.99%
  > 24x7
  > P1 response <5 min
  > Tier 2 — Business Critical
  > 
  > Examples:
  > 
  > Claims Agent
  > Customer Service Agent
  > 
  > Targets:
  > 
  > 99.9%
  > Tier 3 — Internal Productivity
  > 
  > Examples:
  > 
  > Knowledge Agent
  > HR Agent
  > 
  > Targets:
  > 
  > 99%
  > Missing Enterprise Tables
  > Table	Purpose
  > sla_breach	Breach tracking
  > sla_history	Trend analysis
  > sla_exception	Approved waivers
  > sla_review	Quarterly review
  > sla_dependency_mapping	Dependency SLA alignment
  > sla_report	Executive reporting
  > sla_penalty	Vendor penalties
  > sla_alert	Real-time alerts
  > Insurance Example
  > Claims Agent
  > 
  > Business Requirement:
  > 
  > Claim summary available within 5 sec
  > 
  > SLA:
  > 
  > Latency < 5 sec
  > Availability 99.95%
  > Accuracy > 95%
  > Fraud Agent
  > 
  > Business Requirement:
  > 
  > Real-time fraud scoring
  > 
  > SLA:
  > 
  > Latency < 2 sec
  > Availability 99.99%
  > Accuracy > 98%
  > Flowchart
  > Agent Runs
  >       ↓
  > Collect Metrics
  >       ↓
  > Compare SLA
  >       ↓
  > Breach?
  >  ┌────┴────┐
  >  No        Yes
  >  ↓         ↓
  > Green    Create Alert
  >             ↓
  >       Notify Team
  >             ↓
  >       Open Incident
  >             ↓
  >       Track MTTR
  > Brutal Production Reality
  > 
  > The biggest mistake in AgentOps:
  > 
  > Building agents without defining SLAs
  > 
  > Enterprise leadership ultimately cares about:
  > 
  > Availability
  > Accuracy
  > Latency
  > Cost
  > Recovery
  > Risk
  > 
  > not how sophisticated the agent architecture is.
  > 
  > Next Enterprise Table
  > 
  > After agent_sla, the next critical table is:
  > 
  > 28. agent_capacity
  > 
  > This answers:
  > 
  > How many users can the agent support?
  > How many concurrent requests?
  > How much GPU?
  > How much memory?
  > When should it autoscale?
  > When will it fail under load?
  > 
  > This is where AgentOps meets Platform Engineering, Kubernetes, vLLM, Temporal, Kafka, and production scalability.28. agent_capacity — Capacity Planning / Scalability / Concurrency Table
  > 
  > This table answers:
  > 
  > How much workload can this agent handle?
  > When will it break?
  > When should it scale?
  > How many users can it support?
  > How many concurrent executions?
  > 
  > This is where:
  > 
  > AgentOps
  > +
  > Platform Engineering
  > +
  > Kubernetes
  > +
  > vLLM
  > +
  > Kafka
  > +
  > Temporal
  > +
  > GPU
  > 
  > come together.
  > 
  > Why This Table Exists
  > 
  > Most Agent PoCs support:
  > 
  > 10 Users
  > 
  > Production requires:
  > 
  > 1,000 Users
  > 10,000 Users
  > 100,000 Users
  > 
  > Without capacity planning:
  > 
  > CPU Exhaustion
  > GPU Exhaustion
  > Queue Overflow
  > OOM Errors
  > Timeouts
  > Cost Explosion
  > Purpose
  > Area	Purpose
  > Main Purpose	Capacity Planning
  > Operations	Scaling
  > Infrastructure	Resource Planning
  > Performance	Concurrency Control
  > FinOps	Cost Optimization
  > Reliability	Prevent Overload
  > Relationship
  > agent_registry
  >       ↓
  > agent_capacity
  >       ↓
  > agent_health
  >       ↓
  > agent_sla
  >       ↓
  > agent_cost
  > 
  > Connected To:
  > 
  > kubernetes
  > vllm
  > temporal
  > redis
  > kafka
  > gpu_cluster
  > SQL Table
  > CREATE TABLE agent_capacity (
  > 
  >     capacity_id VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
  > 
  >     agent_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     max_concurrent_requests INT,
  > 
  >     max_queue_depth INT,
  > 
  >     max_tokens_per_request INT,
  > 
  >     max_memory_mb INT,
  > 
  >     max_cpu_cores INT,
  > 
  >     max_gpu_memory_mb INT,
  > 
  >     autoscale_min_instances INT,
  > 
  >     autoscale_max_instances INT,
  > 
  >     autoscale_trigger VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     target_latency_ms INT,
  > 
  >     target_throughput_rps INT,
  > 
  >     current_utilization DECIMAL(5,2),
  > 
  >     status VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     last_capacity_test TIMESTAMP,
  > 
  >     created_at TIMESTAMP
  > );
  > Capacity Layers
  > User Capacity
  >       ↓
  > Agent Capacity
  >       ↓
  > Model Capacity
  >       ↓
  > Tool Capacity
  >       ↓
  > MCP Capacity
  >       ↓
  > Infrastructure Capacity
  > Capacity Components
  > Component	Example
  > Concurrent Users	1000
  > Concurrent Sessions	500
  > Requests Per Second	200
  > Tokens Per Minute	2M
  > Queue Size	5000
  > CPU	32 Core
  > RAM	128 GB
  > GPU	A100
  > Storage	10 TB
  > Kafka Throughput	1M Msgs
  > Example
  > Claims Agent
  > Metric	Value
  > Concurrent Users	500
  > Queue Depth	1000
  > RPS	100
  > Avg Tokens	5K
  > GPU Memory	24 GB
  > Capacity Formula
  > 
  > Users:
  > 
  > 1000
  > 
  > Average Requests:
  > 
  > 2/min
  > 
  > Total:
  > 
  > Requests Per Minute=Users×Requests Per User
  > 
  > Result:
  > 
  > 2000 RPM
  > Runtime Flow
  > Request Arrives
  >       ↓
  > Capacity Check
  >       ↓
  > Available?
  >  ┌────┴────┐
  >  Yes       No
  >  ↓         ↓
  > Execute   Queue
  >               ↓
  >          Autoscale
  >               ↓
  >          Retry
  > Capacity States
  > State	Meaning
  > Underutilized	Capacity Available
  > Healthy	Within Limits
  > Busy	Near Capacity
  > Saturated	Full
  > Overloaded	Failure Risk
  > Scaling	Expanding
  > Autoscaling Rules
  > 
  > Example:
  > 
  > CPU > 70%
  > 
  > ↓
  > 
  > Scale
  > 
  > Queue > 500
  > 
  > ↓
  > 
  > Scale
  > 
  > Latency > 5 sec
  > 
  > ↓
  > 
  > Scale
  > 
  > Autoscale Table Example
  > Metric	Threshold
  > CPU	70%
  > Memory	75%
  > Queue Depth	500
  > RPS	100
  > GPU Usage	80%
  > Latency	5 sec
  > Production Issues
  > 1. Queue Explosion
  > 100 Users
  > 
  > ↓
  > 
  > 10,000 Users
  > 
  > ↓
  > 
  > Queue Overflow
  > 
  > Solution:
  > 
  > Kafka
  > Autoscaling
  > 2. GPU Exhaustion
  > LLM Uses
  > 24 GB
  > 
  > GPU:
  > 
  > 24 GB
  > 
  > No capacity left.
  > 
  > Need:
  > 
  > vLLM
  > KV Cache
  > Tensor Parallelism
  > 3. Memory Leak
  > 
  > Agent memory grows forever.
  > 
  > Need:
  > 
  > TTL
  > Cleanup
  > 4. MCP Bottleneck
  > 
  > Agent:
  > 
  > 1000 Requests
  > 
  > MCP:
  > 
  > 50 Requests
  > 
  > Bottleneck.
  > 
  > Need:
  > 
  > Dependency Capacity Tracking
  > 5. Retry Storm
  > 
  > Tool fails.
  > 
  > Agent retries:
  > 
  > 1000 Times
  > 
  > Need:
  > 
  > Circuit Breaker
  > 6. Cost Explosion
  > 
  > Autoscale:
  > 
  > 200 Pods
  > 
  > Cost:
  > 
  > $50,000/month
  > 
  > Need:
  > 
  > Cost Limits
  > Capacity Planning Matrix
  > Users	Nodes	GPU	Redis	Kafka
  > 100	2	1	Small	Small
  > 1000	5	2	Medium	Medium
  > 10000	20	8	Large	Large
  > 100000	100+	40+	Cluster	Cluster
  > Monitoring
  > Metric	Target
  > CPU Usage	<70%
  > Memory Usage	<75%
  > GPU Usage	<80%
  > Queue Depth	<500
  > RPS	SLA
  > Throughput	SLA
  > Token Rate	SLA
  > Scaling Events	Track
  > Supervisor Agent Checks
  > 
  > Before Execution:
  > 
  > Capacity Available?
  > 
  > ↓
  > 
  > Queue Full?
  > 
  > ↓
  > 
  > Scale Needed?
  > 
  > ↓
  > 
  > Fallback Needed?
  > Real Insurance Example
  > 
  > Claims Agent
  > 
  > Expected:
  > 
  > 5000 Claims/Day
  > 
  > Peak:
  > 
  > Monday Morning
  > 
  > Capacity:
  > 
  > 10 Pods
  > 2 GPUs
  > Redis Cluster
  > Kafka Cluster
  > 
  > Autoscale:
  > 
  > 10 → 25 Pods
  > Capacity Testing
  > 
  > Must Perform
  > 
  > Test	Purpose
  > Load Test	Normal Traffic
  > Stress Test	Break Point
  > Spike Test	Sudden Load
  > Soak Test	Long Duration
  > Chaos Test	Failure Recovery
  > Failover Test	DR Validation
  > Missing Enterprise Tables
  > Table	Purpose
  > capacity_forecast	Growth Prediction
  > capacity_test	Load Testing Results
  > capacity_autoscale	Scaling Rules
  > capacity_queue	Queue Monitoring
  > capacity_gpu	GPU Tracking
  > capacity_cost	Capacity Cost
  > capacity_bottleneck	Bottleneck Detection
  > capacity_trend	Growth Trend
  > capacity_alert	Threshold Alerts
  > capacity_planner	Future Planning
  > Capacity Flowchart
  > User Request
  >       ↓
  > Check Capacity
  >       ↓
  > Capacity Available?
  >  ┌────┴────┐
  >  Yes       No
  >  ↓         ↓
  > Run      Queue
  >             ↓
  >       Autoscale
  >             ↓
  >       Capacity Available?
  >          ↓
  >       Execute
  > Brutal Production Reality
  > 
  > Most Agent platforms fail because of:
  > 
  > Issue	Frequency
  > Queue Overflow	Very High
  > GPU Exhaustion	High
  > MCP Bottleneck	High
  > Redis Saturation	High
  > Kafka Backlog	Medium
  > Model Throughput	High
  > Memory Leak	Medium
  > Cost Explosion	High
  > 
  > Not because:
  > 
  > Prompt Engineering
  > 
  > but because:
  > 
  > Capacity Planning
  > 
  > fails.
  > 
  > Enterprise AgentOps Roadmap (28 Tables)
  > 
  > You now have:
  > 
  > Registry Layer
  > --------------
  > agent_registry
  > skill_registry
  > tool_registry
  > mcp_server_registry
  > 
  > Mapping Layer
  > -------------
  > agent_skill_mapping
  > agent_tool_mapping
  > agent_mcp_mapping
  > 
  > Execution Layer
  > ---------------
  > agent_flow
  > agent_invocation
  > agent_memory
  > 
  > Quality Layer
  > -------------
  > agent_scorecard
  > agent_evaluation
  > agent_feedback
  > 
  > Governance Layer
  > ----------------
  > approval_request
  > agent_guardrail
  > agent_security
  > agent_policy
  > 
  > Operations Layer
  > ----------------
  > agent_health
  > agent_state
  > agent_trace
  > agent_incident
  > 
  > Platform Layer
  > --------------
  > agent_version
  > agent_router
  > agent_dependency
  > agent_team
  > agent_sla
  > agent_capacity
  > 
  > FinOps Layer
  > ------------
  > agent_cost
  > 
  > Next logical table:
  > 
  > 29. agent_queue
  > 
  > This is the heart of:
  > 
  > Temporal
  > Kafka
  > RabbitMQ
  > Azure Service Bus
  > AWS SQS
  > Google Pub/Sub
  > 
  > and answers:
  > 
  > Which work is waiting?
  > Which work is running?
  > Which work is stuck?
  > Which work should run next?28. agent_capacity — Capacity Planning / Scalability / Concurrency Table
  > 
  > This table answers:
  > 
  > How much workload can this agent handle?
  > When will it break?
  > When should it scale?
  > How many users can it support?
  > How many concurrent executions?
  > 
  > This is where:
  > 
  > AgentOps
  > +
  > Platform Engineering
  > +
  > Kubernetes
  > +
  > vLLM
  > +
  > Kafka
  > +
  > Temporal
  > +
  > GPU
  > 
  > come together.
  > 
  > Why This Table Exists
  > 
  > Most Agent PoCs support:
  > 
  > 10 Users
  > 
  > Production requires:
  > 
  > 1,000 Users
  > 10,000 Users
  > 100,000 Users
  > 
  > Without capacity planning:
  > 
  > CPU Exhaustion
  > GPU Exhaustion
  > Queue Overflow
  > OOM Errors
  > Timeouts
  > Cost Explosion
  > Purpose
  > Area	Purpose
  > Main Purpose	Capacity Planning
  > Operations	Scaling
  > Infrastructure	Resource Planning
  > Performance	Concurrency Control
  > FinOps	Cost Optimization
  > Reliability	Prevent Overload
  > Relationship
  > agent_registry
  >       ↓
  > agent_capacity
  >       ↓
  > agent_health
  >       ↓
  > agent_sla
  >       ↓
  > agent_cost
  > 
  > Connected To:
  > 
  > kubernetes
  > vllm
  > temporal
  > redis
  > kafka
  > gpu_cluster
  > SQL Table
  > CREATE TABLE agent_capacity (
  > 
  >     capacity_id VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
  > 
  >     agent_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     max_concurrent_requests INT,
  > 
  >     max_queue_depth INT,
  > 
  >     max_tokens_per_request INT,
  > 
  >     max_memory_mb INT,
  > 
  >     max_cpu_cores INT,
  > 
  >     max_gpu_memory_mb INT,
  > 
  >     autoscale_min_instances INT,
  > 
  >     autoscale_max_instances INT,
  > 
  >     autoscale_trigger VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     target_latency_ms INT,
  > 
  >     target_throughput_rps INT,
  > 
  >     current_utilization DECIMAL(5,2),
  > 
  >     status VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     last_capacity_test TIMESTAMP,
  > 
  >     created_at TIMESTAMP
  > );
  > Capacity Layers
  > User Capacity
  >       ↓
  > Agent Capacity
  >       ↓
  > Model Capacity
  >       ↓
  > Tool Capacity
  >       ↓
  > MCP Capacity
  >       ↓
  > Infrastructure Capacity
  > Capacity Components
  > Component	Example
  > Concurrent Users	1000
  > Concurrent Sessions	500
  > Requests Per Second	200
  > Tokens Per Minute	2M
  > Queue Size	5000
  > CPU	32 Core
  > RAM	128 GB
  > GPU	A100
  > Storage	10 TB
  > Kafka Throughput	1M Msgs
  > Example
  > Claims Agent
  > Metric	Value
  > Concurrent Users	500
  > Queue Depth	1000
  > RPS	100
  > Avg Tokens	5K
  > GPU Memory	24 GB
  > Capacity Formula
  > 
  > Users:
  > 
  > 1000
  > 
  > Average Requests:
  > 
  > 2/min
  > 
  > Total:
  > 
  > Requests Per Minute=Users×Requests Per User
  > 
  > Result:

  > Stress Test	Break Point
  > Spike Test	Sudden Load
  > Soak Test	Long Duration
  > Chaos Test	Failure Recovery
  > Failover Test	DR Validation

  > Check Capacity

  >  Yes       No
  >  ↓         ↓
  > Run      Queue

  >          ↓

  > Next logical table:
  > 
  > 29. agent_queue
  > 
  > This is the heart of:
  > 
  > Temporal
  > Kafka
  > RabbitMQ
  > Azure Service Bus
  > AWS SQS
  > Google Pub/Sub
  > 
  > and answers:
  > 
  > Which work is waiting?
  > Which work is running?
  > Which work is stuck?
  > Which work should run next?29. agent_queue — Work Queue / Task Scheduling Table
  > 
  > This table answers:
  > 
  > What work is waiting?
  > What work is running?
  > What is stuck?
  > What failed?
  > What should run next?
  > 
  > This table is critical when your platform moves from:
  > 
  > single agent demo
  > 
  > to:
  > 
  > production multi-agent automation
  > Why This Table Exists
  > 
  > Bad design:
  > 
  > User request directly triggers agent
  > 
  > Problem:
  > 
  > 1000 requests arrive at once
  > Agent crashes
  > Database overloads
  > LLM rate limit hits
  > 
  > Good design:
  > 
  > Request → Queue → Worker → Agent Execution → Result
  > Purpose
  > Area	Purpose
  > Main Purpose	Manage pending/running agent work
  > Scalability	Handle traffic spikes
  > Reliability	Retry failed jobs
  > Operations	Detect stuck jobs
  > Governance	Prioritize critical work
  > Cost Control	Throttle expensive jobs
  > Relationship
  > agent_flow
  >    ↓
  > agent_queue
  >    ↓
  > agent_invocation
  >    ↓
  > agent_trace
  > 
  > Connected to:
  > 
  > agent_capacity
  > agent_sla
  > agent_cost
  > agent_policy
  > agent_health
  > SQL Table
  > CREATE TABLE agent_queue (
  >     queue_id VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
  > 
  >     job_id VARCHAR(100),
  >     flow_id VARCHAR(100),
  >     agent_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     job_type VARCHAR(100),
  >     priority INT,
  > 
  >     payload JSONB,
  > 
  >     queue_status VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     retry_count INT DEFAULT 0,
  >     max_retries INT DEFAULT 3,
  > 
  >     scheduled_at TIMESTAMP,
  >     started_at TIMESTAMP,
  >     completed_at TIMESTAMP,
  > 
  >     locked_by VARCHAR(100),
  >     lock_expires_at TIMESTAMP,
  > 
  >     error_message TEXT,
  > 
  >     created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
  > );
  > Queue Status
  > Status	Meaning
  > Pending	Waiting
  > Running	Being processed
  > Completed	Finished
  > Failed	Failed permanently
  > Retrying	Waiting retry
  > Delayed	Scheduled later
  > Stuck	Running too long
  > Cancelled	Manually stopped
  > Dead Letter	Cannot process
  > Runtime Flow
  > Event/User Request
  >       ↓
  > Create Queue Job
  >       ↓
  > Worker Picks Job
  >       ↓
  > Lock Job
  >       ↓
  > Run Agent Flow
  >       ↓
  > Create Invocation
  >       ↓
  > Complete / Retry / Fail
  > Example
  > 
  > Incident created:
  > 
  > Payment API down
  > 
  > Queue job:
  > 
  > {
  >   "job_id": "job_1001",
  >   "agent_id": "incident_triage_agent",
  >   "job_type": "incident_triage",
  >   "priority": 1,
  >   "queue_status": "Pending",
  >   "max_retries": 3
  > }
  > Priority Rules
  > Priority	Meaning	Example
  > 1	Critical	P1 incident
  > 2	High	Security alert
  > 3	Medium	Claims review
  > 4	Low	Daily report
  > 5	Background	Data enrichment
  > Production Issues
  > Issue	Example	Solution
  > Queue overflow	Too many requests	autoscale workers
  > Stuck jobs	Worker dies mid-run	lock timeout
  > Duplicate jobs	Same event twice	idempotency key
  > Retry storm	Failing job retries nonstop	backoff + circuit breaker
  > Starvation	Low priority never runs	fairness policy
  > Dead letters ignored	Failed jobs pile up	DLQ review
  > Wrong priority	P1 treated as low	priority policy
  > No vi
  > 
  > [Message truncated - exceeded 50,000 character limit]

  > 65. business_value_stream — Enterprise Value Stream Architecture
  > 
  > This is the highest-level business architecture object before strategy.
  > 
  > Most companies organize around:
  > 
  > Departments
  > Teams
  > Applications
  > Projects
  > 
  > World-class organizations organize around:
  > 
  > Value Streams
  > What Is A Value Stream?
  > 
  > A Value Stream answers:
  > 
  > How does value flow from customer request
  > to customer outcome?
  > 
  > Example:
  > 
  > Customer submits claim.
  > 
  > Value stream:
  > 
  > Customer
  >      ↓
  > Submit Claim
  >      ↓
  > Validate Claim
  >      ↓
  > Assess Damage
  >      ↓
  > Detect Fraud
  >      ↓
  > Approve Claim
  >      ↓
  > Issue Payment
  >      ↓
  > Customer Receives Money
  > 
  > This entire flow is:
  > 
  > Claims Value Stream
  > Why Value Streams Exist
  > 
  > Without Value Streams
  > 
  > Organization thinks:
  > 
  > HR
  > Claims
  > IT
  > Fraud
  > Compliance
  > Finance
  > 
  > Silos.
  > 
  > With Value Streams
  > 
  > Organization thinks:
  > 
  > Customer Outcome
  > Enterprise Hierarchy
  > Corporate Strategy
  >       ↓
  > Value Stream
  >       ↓
  > Capability
  >       ↓
  > Process
  >       ↓
  > Subprocess
  >       ↓
  > Workflow
  >       ↓
  > Agent
  >       ↓
  > Execution

  > Main Purpose	Customer Value Delivery

  > Need:
  > 
  > Enterprise Shared Services
  > 5. Department KPI Conflict
  > 
  > Fraud KPI:
  > 
  > Investigate More Claims
  > 
  > Claims KPI:
  > 
  > Settle Claims Faster
  > 
  > Conflict.
  > 
  > Need:
  > 
  > Value Stream Alignment
  > 6. AI Ownership Gap
  > 
  > Agent deployed.
  > 
  > Question:
  > 
  > Who maintains prompts?
  > 
  > Who retrains?
  > 
  > Who monitors?
  > 
  > Unknown.
  >

  >

  > Enterprise Operating Model
  > Strategy
  >    ↓
  > Value Stream
  >    ↓
  > Department
  >    ↓
  > Capability
  >    ↓
  > Process
  >    ↓
  > Subprocess
  >    ↓
  > Program
  >    ↓
  > Project
  >    ↓
  > Agent
  > Brutal Reality
  > 
  > Most AI transformations fail because:
  > 
  > Technology Has Owners
  > 
  > Business Value Does Not
  > 
  > Successful enterprises assign:
  > 
  > Department Owner
  > 
  > Executive Owner
  > 
  > Budget Owner
  > 
  > KPI Owner
  > 
  > Agent Owner
  > 
  > Risk Owner
  > 
  > before building AI.
  > 
  > Next: 67. team
  > 
  > This moves from organizational ownership to delivery ownership:
  > 
  > Department
  >       ↓
  > Team
  >       ↓
  > Role
  >       ↓
  > Person
  > 
  > and answers:
  > 
  > Which team builds the agent?
  > 
  > Which team supports it?
  > 
  > Which team owns MCP?
  > 
  > Which team owns RAG?
  > 
  > Which team handles incidents?
  > 
  > Which team manages production?
  > 67. team — Enterprise Delivery Team / AgentOps Ownership Model
  > 
  > This is where organizational ownership becomes operational ownership.
  > 
  > Department owns the business.
  > 
  > Team delivers and operates the solution.
  > 
  > This table answers:
  > 
  > Who builds the agent?
  > 
  > Who supports the agent?
  > 
  > Who owns the MCP?
  > 
  > Who owns RAG?
  > 
  > Who manages incidents?
  > 
  > Who handles production outages?
  > 
  > Who upgrades models?
  > 
  > Who retrains AI?
  > Enterprise Hierarchy
  > Enterprise
  >     ↓
  > Business Unit
  >     ↓
  > Department
  >     ↓
  > Team
  >     ↓
  > Role
  >     ↓
  > Person
  > Why Team Exists
  > 
  > Department:
  > 
  > Claims Department
  > 
  > Cannot directly operate:
  > 
  > 100 Agents
  > 
  > 50 MCPs
  > 
  > 20 RAG Systems
  > 
  > 10 LLM Platforms
  > 
  > Need specialized teams.
  > 
  > Purpose
  > Area	Purpose
  > Main Purpose	Delivery Ownership
  > Operations	Production Support
  > DevOps	Deployment
  > AgentOps	Agent Management
  > MLOps	Model Operations
  > Governance	Accountability

  > SQL Table
  > CREATE TABLE team (
  > 
  >     team_id VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
  > 
  >     department_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     team_name VARCHAR(300),
  > 
  >     team_type VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     manager_name VARCHAR(200),
  > 
  >     technical_lead VARCHAR(200),
  > 
  >     team_size INT,
  > 
  >     primary_responsibility TEXT,
  > 
  >     support_level VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     on_call_enabled BOOLEAN,
  > 
  >     budget DECIMAL(15,2),
  > 
  >     maturity_level VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     status VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     created_at TIMESTAMP
  > );

  > Brutal Reality
  > 
  > Most AI programs fail because they assign ownership to:
  > 
  > Departments
  > 
  > Successful AI programs assign ownership to:
  > 
  > Operational Teams
  > 
  > because teams—not departments—actually build, deploy, monitor, support, secure, and improve AI systems.

  > Agent Architect

  > AI Program Manager
  > 68. role — Enterprise AI Role Catalog / Workforce Architecture
  > 
  > This is one of the most important tables in the entire Enterprise AI Operating Model.
  > 
  > Without role definition:
  > 
  > Everyone Builds
  > 
  > Nobody Owns
  > 
  > Nobody Approves
  > 
  > Nobody Supports
  > 
  > Chaos.
  > 
  > Why Role Exists
  > 
  > Question:
  > 
  > Who can create an agent?
  > 
  > Who can approve production?
  > 
  > Who can change prompts?
  > 
  > Who can modify MCP?
  > 
  > Who can access memory?
  > 
  > Who can approve AI policies?
  > 
  > Answer comes from:
  > 
  > Role
  > Enterprise Hierarchy
  > Enterprise
  >       ↓
  > Department
  >       ↓
  > Team
  >       ↓
  > Role
  >       ↓
  > User
  >       ↓
  > Permission
  > Purpose
  > Area	Purpose
  > Main Purpose	Responsibility Definition
  > HR	Job Architecture
  > Security	RBAC
  > Governance	Accountability
  > PMO	Resource Planning
  > Operations	Ownership
  > Relationship
  > department
  >       ↓
  > team
  >       ↓
  > role
  >       ↓
  > user
  > 
  > Connected To
  > 
  > team
  > user
  > rbac_policy
  > approval_request
  > agent_registry
  > agent_flow
  > agent_release
  > agent_incident
  > SQL Table
  > CREATE TABLE role (
  > 
  >     role_id VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
  > 
  >     team_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     role_name VARCHAR(300),
  > 
  >     role_category VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     role_description TEXT,
  > 
  >     seniority_level VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     primary_responsibilities TEXT,
  > 
  >     required_skills TEXT,
  > 
  >     required_certifications TEXT,
  > 
  >     approval_authority BOOLEAN,
  > 
  >     production_access BOOLEAN,
  > 
  >     on_call_eligible BOOLEAN,
  > 
  >     status VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     created_at TIMESTAMP
  > );

  > Production Issues
  > 1. Role Overlap
  > 
  > Agent Architect
  > 
  > and
  > 
  > Solution Architect
  > 
  > Both doing same work.
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Clear Responsibilities
  > 2. Missing Role
  > 
  > Nobody assigned:
  > 
  > AgentOps
  > 
  > Result:
  > 
  > No Monitoring
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Operations Ownership
  > 3. Excessive Permissions
  > 
  > Developer can:
  > 
  > Deploy To Production
  > 
  > Risk.
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > RBAC
  > 4. Approval Bottleneck
  > 
  > One Architect approves:
  > 
  > 500 Requests
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Delegation Model
  > 5. Skill Gap
  > 
  > Need:
  > 
  > MCP
  > 
  > RAG
  > 
  > LLMOps
  > 
  > No expertise.
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Training Plan
  > 6. Shadow Roles
  > 
  > Employee acts as:
  > 
  > Prompt Engineer
  > 
  > Not officially defined.
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Role Catalog
  > 7. No Succession Planning
  > 
  > Expert leaves.
  > 
  > Knowledge lost.
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Backup Resource
  > Role Dashboard
  > Executive
  > Metric	Value
  > Roles	85
  > AI Roles	24
  > Critical Roles	12
  > Vacant Roles	3
  > AgentOps Team
  > Role	Count
  > AgentOps Engineer	8
  > Senior AgentOps Engineer	3
  > Lead AgentOps Engineer	1
  > Monitoring
  > Metric	Target
  > Role Coverage	100%
  > Skill Coverage	100%
  > Backup Coverage	>90%
  > Critical Vacancies	0
  > Training Completion	High
  > Certification Coverage	High
  > Supervisor Agent Checks
  > Role Defined?
  > 
  > ↓
  > 
  > Responsibilities Defined?
  > 
  > ↓
  >

  > Next: 69. responsibility_matrix (RACI)
  > 
  > This is where every:
  > 
  > Capability
  > Process
  > Agent

  > Release

  > Consulted (C)
  > 
  > Informed (I)
  > 69. responsibility_matrix (RACI) — Enterprise Accountability & Governance Model
  > 
  > This is one of the most critical governance tables in Enterprise AI.
  > 
  > Most production incidents occur because:
  > 
  > Everybody Thought
  > Somebody Else Owned It
  > 
  > RACI eliminates ambiguity.
  > 
  > What Is RACI?
  > Letter	Meaning
  > R	Responsible
  > A	Accountable
  > C	Consulted
  > I	Informed
  > Simple Example
  > 
  > Fraud Agent Production Release
  > 
  > Role	RACI
  > AgentOps Engineer	R
  > AI Architect	C
  > Security Lead	C
  > Product Owner	I
  > Fraud Director	A
  > 
  > Meaning
  > 
  > AgentOps Executes
  > 
  > Fraud Director Owns Outcome
  > 
  > Architect Consulted
  > 
  > Product Owner Informed
  > Why RACI Exists
  > 
  > Without RACI
  > 
  > Production Incident:
  > 
  > Fraud Agent Down
  > 
  > Questions:
  > 
  > Who fixes it?
  > 
  > Who approves?
  > 
  > Who escalates?
  > 
  > Who informs business?
  > 
  > Nobody knows.
  > 
  > With RACI
  > 
  > Everything predefined.
  > 
  > Enterprise Hierarchy
  > Strategy
  >     ↓
  > Department
  >     ↓
  > Team
  >     ↓
  > Role
  >     ↓
  > RACI
  >     ↓
  > Execution
  > Purpose
  > Area	Purpose
  > Main Purpose	Accountability
  > Governance	Ownership
  > Security	Approval
  > Operations	Escalation
  > PMO	Delivery Accountability
  > Audit	Evidence
  > Relationship
  > department
  >       ↓
  > team
  >       ↓
  > role
  >       ↓
  > responsibility_matrix
  > 
  > Connected To
  > 
  > business_capability
  > 
  > business_process
  > 
  > agent_registry
  > 
  > skill_registry
  > 
  > mcp_server_registry
  > 
  > agent_flow
  > 
  > agent_release
  > 
  > agent_incident
  > 
  > project_risk
  > SQL Table
  > CREATE TABLE responsibility_matrix (
  > 
  >     raci_id VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
  > 
  >     object_type VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     object_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     role_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     responsibility_type VARCHAR(10),
  > 
  >     responsibility_description TEXT,
  > 
  >     escalation_level INT,
  > 
  >     approval_required BOOLEAN,
  > 
  >     effective_date DATE,
  > 
  >     expiry_date DATE,
  > 
  >     status VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     created_at TIMESTAMP
  > );

  >

  > Prompt Changes
  > Role	RACI

  > Governance Team	I

  > LLMOps Engineer	R

  > Runtime Flow
  > Request
  >    ↓
  > RACI Lookup
  >    ↓
  > Responsible Found
  >    ↓
  > Accountable Found
  >    ↓
  > Approval Flow
  >    ↓
  > Execution
  > Escalation Flow
  > 
  > Severity 1 Incident
  > 
  > AgentOps Engineer
  >         ↓
  > AgentOps Lead
  >         ↓
  > Director
  >         ↓
  > VP
  >         ↓
  > Executive Sponsor
  > Production Issues
  > 1. Multiple Accountable People
  > 
  > Bad
  > 
  > 2 Accountable Owners
  > 
  > Chaos.
  > 
  > Rule:
  > 
  > One Accountable Person
  > 
  > Always.
  > 
  > 2. No Responsible Owner
  > 
  > Task exists.
  > 
  > Nobody executes.
  > 
  > Need:
  > 
  > At Least One R
  > 3. Too Many Consulted
  > 
  > Meeting:
  > 
  > 50 People
  > 
  > No decision.
  > 
  > Need:
  > 
  > Minimal Consulted
  > 4. Missing Incident RACI
  > 
  > Production Failure.
  > 
  > Escalation unclear.
  > 
  > Need:
  > 
  > Incident RACI
  > 5. Approval Conflicts
  > 
  > Architect approves.
  > 
  > Security rejects.
  > 
  > Need:
  > 
  > Approval Hierarchy
  > 6. RACI Drift
  > 
  > Document says:
  > 
  > Team A
  > 
  > Reality:
  > 
  > Team B
  > 
  > Need:
  > 
  > Quarterly Review
  > 7. Shadow Ownership
  > 
  > Agent deployed.
  > 
  > No RACI.
  > 
  > Need:
  > 
  > Mandatory Registration
  > Enterprise AI RACI Framework
  > Agent Lifecycle
  > Activity	R	A	C	I
  > Design	Agent Architect	AI Director	Security	Product
  > Build	Agent Developer	Engineering Lead	Architect	Product
  > Test	QA	QA Lead	Product	Business
  > Deploy	AgentOps	AgentOps Lead	Security	Business
  > Monitor	AgentOps	AgentOps Lead	Product	CIO
  > Retire	AgentOps	Product Owner	Governance	CIO
  > Monitoring
  > Metric	Target
  > RACI Coverage	100%
  > Objects Without Owner	0
  > Multiple Accountable Owners	0
  > Escalation Accuracy	High
  > Audit Compliance	100%
  > Incident Ownership	100%

  > Department
  >       ↓

  > Brutal Reality
  > 
  > Most enterprise AI failures are not caused by:
  > 
  > GPT
  > 
  > Claude
  >

  > They are caused by:
  > 
  > No Ownership
  > 
  > No Accountability
  > 
  > No Escalation
  > 
  > No Governance

  > RACI is the mechanism that turns an AI platform into an auditable enterprise operating model.

  > Next: 70. stakeholder
  > 
  > This becomes the stakeholder governance model and tracks:
  > 
  > Executive Sponsors
  > 
  > Business Owners
  > 
  > Product Owners
  > 
  > Department Heads
  > 
  > Compliance Officers
  > 
  > Security Officers
  > 
  > Architects
  > 
  > Vendors
  > 
  > Regulators
  > 
  > including:
  > 
  > Influence
  > Interest
  > Decision Power
  > Funding Authority
  > Approval Rights
  > Communication Plans
  > Stakeholder Risk
  > 70. stakeholder — Enterprise Stakeholder Governance & Influence Management
  > 
  > This is one of the most underestimated tables in Enterprise AI.
  > 
  > Most AI projects fail because of:
  > 
  > Technology Problems = 20%
  > 
  > People Problems = 80%
  > 
  > This table answers:
  > 
  > Who funds the project?
  > 
  > Who can stop the project?
  > 
  > Who approves the project?
  > 
  > Who influences the project?
  > 
  > Who must be consulted?
  > 
  > Who must be informed?
  > 
  > Who are the project champions?
  > 
  > Who are the project blockers?
  > Why Stakeholder Management Exists
  > 
  > AI Team thinks:
  > 
  > Build Agent
  > Deploy Agent
  > Done
  > 
  > Reality:
  > 
  > Executive Sponsor
  > Business Owner
  > Security
  > Compliance
  > Architecture Board
  > Operations
  > Legal
  > Risk
  > Audit
  > 
  > must all align.
  > 
  > Enterprise Hierarchy
  > Strategy
  >     ↓
  > Stakeholder
  >     ↓
  > Department
  >     ↓
  > Team
  >     ↓
  > Role
  >     ↓
  > User
  > Purpose
  > Area	Purpose
  > Main Purpose	Stakeholder Governance
  > PMO	Stakeholder Tracking
  > Executive	Sponsorship
  > Governance	Approval
  > Change Management	Adoption
  > Risk	Resistance Management
  > Relationship
  > stakeholder
  >       ↓
  > department
  >       ↓
  > team
  >       ↓
  > role
  > 
  > Connected To
  > 
  > agent_program
  > agent_project
  > business_capability
  > business_process
  > project_risk
  > approval_request
  > responsibility_matrix
  > SQL Table
  > CREATE TABLE stakeholder (
  > 
  >     stakeholder_id VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
  > 
  >     stakeholder_name VARCHAR(300),
  > 
  >     stakeholder_type VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     department_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     role_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     influence_level VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     interest_level VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     decision_authority BOOLEAN,
  > 
  >     funding_authority BOOLEAN,
  > 
  >     approval_authority BOOLEAN,
  > 
  >     communication_preference VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     stakeholder_risk_score DECIMAL(5,2),
  > 
  >     stakeholder_notes TEXT,
  > 
  >     status VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     created_at TIMESTAMP
  > );
  > Stakeholder Types
  > Type	Example
  > Executive Sponsor	CIO
  > Business Owner	Claims Director
  > Product Owner	Fraud Product Owner
  > Architect	Enterprise Architect
  > Security	Security Director
  > Compliance	Compliance Manager
  > Risk	Risk Officer
  > Operations	Operations Manager
  > Vendor	Microsoft
  > Partner	Consulting Partner
  > Regulator	Insurance Regulator
  > Customer	Policy Holder

  > 
  > Manage Closely
  > 
  > Examples
  > 
  > CAIO
  > 
  > CIO
  >

  > 
  > CFO
  >

  > Low Power + High Interest
  > 
  > Keep Informed
  > 
  > Examples
  > 
  > Users
  > 
  > Analysts
  > 
  > Operations

  >

  > Stakeholder Influence Model
  > Positive Influence
  > Supports AI
  > 
  > Provides Funding
  > 
  > Removes Blockers
  > Neutral Influence
  > Observes
  > 
  > No Strong Opinion
  > Negative Influence
  > Blocks Funding
  > 
  > Delays Approval
  > 
  > Creates Resistance
  > 
  > Need:
  > 
  > Stakeholder Engagement Plan
  > Communication Plan
  > Stakeholder	Frequency
  > CIO	Monthly
  > Executive Sponsor	Weekly
  > Product Owner	Daily
  > Security	Weekly
  > Compliance	Biweekly
  > Operations	Daily
  > Production Issues
  > 1. Missing Executive Sponsor
  > 
  > AI Project
  > 
  > No Sponsor
  > 
  > Result
  > 
  > No Funding
  > 
  > No Escalation
  > 
  > Project Dies
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Executive Sponsor
  > 2. Stakeholder Identified Too Late
  > 
  > Security added:
  > 
  > 1 Week Before Production
  > 
  > Result
  > 
  > Deployment Blocked
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Early Engagement
  > 3. Business Not Involved
  > 
  > AI Team builds.
  > 
  > Business rejects.
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Continuous Engagement
  > 4. Approval Authority Unknown
  > 
  > Question:
  > 
  > Who Approves?
  > 
  > Nobody knows.
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Approval Matrix
  > 5. Communication Failure
  > 
  > Executive expects:
  > 
  > Monthly Updates
  > 
  > Receives:
  > 
  > Nothing
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Communication Plan
  > 6. Stakeholder Conflict
  > 
  > Claims Wants
  > 
  > Fast Settlement
  > 
  > Fraud Wants
  > 
  > More Investigation
  > 
  > Conflict.
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Governance Board
  > 7. Adoption Resistance
  > 
  > Users fear:
  > 
  > AI Replaces Jobs
  > 
  > Result
  > 
  > Low Adoption
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Change Management
  > Stakeholder Dashboard
  > Executive View
  > Metric	Value
  > Stakeholders	320
  > Sponsors	25
  > Decision Makers	48
  > High Influence	32
  > High Risk	8
  > Program View
  > Metric	Value
  > Engaged	95%
  > Communication Compliance	98%
  > Approval Delays	2
  > Stakeholder Risks	3
  > Stakeholder Risk Scoring
  > 
  > Formula
  > 
  > Stakeholder Risk=Influence×Resistance
  > 
  > Example
  > 
  > Influence = 5
  > 
  > Resistance = 4
  > 
  > Risk
  > 
  > 20 (Critical)
  > Monitoring
  > Metric	Target
  > Stakeholder Coverage	100%
  > Sponsor Coverage	100%
  > Communication Compliance	>95%
  > Approval Delays	Low
  > Adoption Rate	High
  > Stakeholder Satisfaction	High

  > 71. ai_policy — Enterprise AI Governance Policy Framework
  > 
  > This is where Enterprise AI Governance officially begins.
  > 
  > Everything before this point was:
  > 
  > Business
  > Organization
  > Projects
  > Agents
  > Skills
  > MCP
  > Teams
  > Roles

  > Now we move into:
  > 
  > Governance
  > Risk
  > Compliance
  > Audit
  > Security
  > Regulation
  > Responsible AI
  > Why AI Policy Exists
  > 
  > Without Policy
  > 
  > Anyone Creates Agent
  >

  > 
  > Conflict.
  >

  > 
  > Reality:
  > 
  > GPT-7 Running
  >

  >

  > Brutal Reality
  > 
  > Most organizations think AI Governance is:
  > 
  > A Document
  > 
  > Enterprise AI Governance is actually:
  > 
  > Policy
  > +
  > Enforcement
  > +
  > Monitoring
  > +
  > Audit
  > +
  > Evidence
  > 
  > A policy that is not enforced is just documentation.
  > 
  > Next: 72. ai_standard
  > 
  > This becomes the mandatory implementation standard layer, defining:
  > 
  > Agent Design Standards
  > 
  > Prompt Standards
  > 
  > RAG Standards
  > 
  > Chunking Standards
  > 
  > Embedding Standards
  > 
  > MCP Standards
  > 
  > Security Standards
  > 
  > Observability Standards
  > 
  > Evaluation Standards
  > 
  > Deployment Standards
  > 
  > and answers:
  > 72. ai_standard — Enterprise AI Implementation Standards
  > 
  > Policies answer:
  > 
  > What is allowed?
  > 
  > Standards answer:
  > 
  > How exactly must it be implemented?
  > Policy vs Standard vs Guideline
  > Layer	Purpose	Mandatory
  > Policy	What must happen	Yes
  > Standard	How it must be implemented	Yes
  > Guideline	Recommended approach	No
  > Best Practice	Preferred approach	No
  > 
  > Example
  > 
  > Policy:
  > 
  > All agents must be observable.
  > 
  > Standard:
  > 
  > OpenTelemetry required.
  > 
  > Trace ID required.
  > 
  > Span ID required.
  > 
  > Agent execution logged.
  > 
  > Guideline:
  > 
  > Use Langfuse for tracing.
  > Why Standards Exist
  > 
  > Without Standards
  > 
  > Team A:
  > 
  > Chunk Size = 500
  > 
  > Team B:
  > 
  > Chunk Size = 5000
  > 
  > Team C:
  > 
  > No Chunking
  > 
  > Result:
  > 
  > Chaos
  > 
  > With Standards
  > 
  > Consistent
  > 
  > Repeatable
  > 
  > Auditable
  > 
  > Scalable
  > Enterprise Governance Hierarchy
  > AI Policy
  >       ↓
  > AI Standard
  >       ↓
  > AI Guideline
  >       ↓
  > Implementation
  > Purpose
  > Area	Purpose
  > Main Purpose	Technical Consistency
  > Architecture	Standardization
  > Governance	Enforcement
  > Operations	Repeatability

  > SQL Table
  > CREATE TABLE ai_standard (
  > 
  >     standard_id VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
  > 
  >     policy_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     standard_name VARCHAR(300),
  > 
  >     standard_category VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     standard_description TEXT,
  > 
  >     implementation_requirements TEXT,
  > 
  >     validation_method TEXT,
  > 
  >     enforcement_level VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     owner VARCHAR(200),
  > 
  >     review_frequency VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     version VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     status VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     created_at TIMESTAMP
  > );

  > Brutal Reality
  > 
  > The most valuable asset in an AI-driven enterprise is not:
  > 
  > Models
  > 
  > Agents
  > 
  > Vector Databases
  > 
  > Cloud Infrastructure
  > 
  > It is:
  > 
  > Institutional Knowledge
  > 
  > Because models change.
  > 
  > Tools change.
  > 
  > Vendors change.
  > 
  > Technology changes.
  > 
  > But organizations that continuously capture, govern, and reuse knowledge build a compounding advantage that becomes their long-term AI moat.
  >

  > These become the enterprise-scale AI Operating System layer above the governance foundati99. lessons_learned — Enterprise Knowledge Management, Organizational Memory & Continuous Improvement Framework
  > 
  > This is where the organization stops repeating mistakes.
  > 
  > Most companies have:
  > 
  > Incidents
  > 
  > RCA
  > 
  > Postmortems
  > 
  > But they fail because:
  > 
  > Lessons stay in documents
  > 
  > Nobody reuses them
  > 
  > Same failures happen again
  > 
  > The purpose of:
  > 
  > lessons_learned
  > 
  > is to make organizational knowledge reusable.
  > 
  > Why Lessons Learned Exists
  > 
  > Incident
  > 
  > Prompt Injection Attack
  > 
  > RCA
  > 
  > Input Validation Missing
  > 
  > Postmortem
  > 
  > Prompt Firewall Needed
  > 
  > Question
  > 
  > How do we ensure future projects
  > don't repeat this mistake?
  > 
  > Answer
  > 
  > Lessons Learned Repository

  > SQL Table
  > CREATE TABLE lessons_learned (
  > 
  >     lesson_id VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
  > 
  >     lesson_title VARCHAR(500),
  > 
  >     lesson_category VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     source_type VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     source_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     lesson_description TEXT,
  > 
  >     root_cause_summary TEXT,
  > 
  >     recommendation TEXT,
  > 
  >     reusable_control TEXT,
  > 
  >     best_practice TEXT,
  > 
  >     anti_pattern TEXT,
  > 
  >     owner VARCHAR(200),
  > 
  >     approval_status VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     lesson_status VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     created_at TIMESTAMP
  > );
  > Lesson Categories
  > Category	Example
  > AI Governance	Hallucination Prevention
  > Security	Prompt Injection
  > AgentOps	Agent Loops
  > MCP Governance	Tool Abuse
  > ModelOps	Drift Prevention
  > Compliance	HITL Failures
  > Operations	Monitoring Gaps
  > Architecture	Scalability Issues
  > Cost Governance	Token Explosion
  > Vendor Governance	SLA Failures
  > Lesson Structure
  > 
  > Every lesson should answer:
  > 
  > What happened?
  > 
  > Why did it happen?
  > 
  > What was learned?
  > 
  > What should be repeated?
  > 
  > What should be avoided?
  > 
  > How can future teams benefit?
  > Hallucination Example
  > 
  > Lesson
  > 
  > RAG Without Reranking
  > Produces High Hallucinations
  > 
  > Root Cause
  > 
  > Low Retrieval Precision
  > 
  > Recommendation
  > 
  > Always Use Reranker
  > For Critical Systems
  > 
  > Best Practice
  > 
  > Hybrid Search + Reranking
  > 
  > Anti-Pattern
  > 
  > Vector Search Only
  > Prompt Injection Example
  > 
  > Lesson
  > 
  > Prompt Shield Required
  > For Public LLM Systems
  > 
  > Recommendation
  > 
  > Mandatory Prompt Firewall
  > 
  > Reusable Control
  > 
  > Prompt Injection Control Set
  > Agent Governance Example
  > 
  > Lesson
  > 
  > Autonomous Approval
  > Requires HITL
  > 
  > Best Practice
  > 
  > Human Approval
  > For High-Risk Actions
  > 
  > Anti-Pattern
  > 
  > Fully Autonomous Approval
  > MCP Governance Example
  > 
  > Lesson
  > 
  > Shared Credentials Cause
  > Unauthorized Actions
  > 
  > Recommendation
  > 
  > RBAC + Secret Rotation
  > 
  > Reusable Control
  > 
  > MCP Access Governance
  > Model Drift Example
  > 
  > Lesson
  > 
  > Quarterly Reviews Are Too Slow
  > 
  > Recommendation
  > 
  > Continuous Drift Monitoring

  >

  > and serves as the organizational brain powering RAG, Copilots, Agents, Governance Platforms, and Enterprise Decision Support Systems.98. incident_postmortem — Enterprise Incident Learning, Executive Review & Organizational Knowledge Framework
  > 
  > An RCA explains:
  > 
  > Why the incident happened
  > 
  > A Postmortem explains:
  > 
  > What the organization learned
  > 
  > What must improve
  > 
  > How recurrence will be prevented
  > 
  > How governance will evolve
  > 
  > This is the final learning stage of the incident lifecycle.
  > 
  > Why Postmortems Exist
  > 
  > Most organizations stop at:
  > 
  > Incident Resolved
  > 
  > Mature organizations continue to:
  > 
  > Learning
  > 
  > Improvement
  > 
  > Governance Maturity
  > Governance Hierarchy
  > Incident
  >       ↓
  > Timeline
  >       ↓
  > RCA
  >       ↓
  > Postmortem
  >       ↓
  > Knowledge Base
  >       ↓
  > Governance Improvement
  > Purpose
  > Area	Purpose
  > Main Purpose	Organizational Learning
  > Governance	Process Improvement
  > Security	Control Enhancement
  > Operations	Reliability Improvement
  > Compliance	Governance Improvement
  > Executive	Strategic Learning
  > Relationship
  > incident_management
  >         ↓
  > incident_rca
  >         ↓
  > incident_postmortem
  > 
  > Connected To
  > 
  > incident_management
  > 
  > incident_rca
  > 
  > lessons_learned
  > 
  > knowledge_base
  > 
  > remediation_plan
  > 
  > audit_finding
  > 
  > risk_register
  > SQL Table
  > CREATE TABLE incident_postmortem (
  > 
  >     postmortem_id VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
  > 
  >     incident_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     postmortem_title VARCHAR(500),
  > 
  >     executive_summary TEXT,
  > 
  >     business_impact TEXT,
  > 
  >     timeline_summary TEXT,
  > 
  >     root_cause_summary TEXT,
  > 
  >     what_went_well TEXT,
  > 
  >     what_went_poorly TEXT,
  > 
  >     lessons_learned TEXT,
  > 
  >     process_improvements TEXT,
  > 
  >     governance_improvements TEXT,
  > 
  >     approved_by VARCHAR(200),
  > 
  >     approval_date DATE,
  > 
  >     created_at TIMESTAMP
  > );
  > Core Questions
  > 
  > Every postmortem should answer:
  > 
  > What happened?
  > 
  > What was the impact?
  > 
  > What worked well?
  > 
  > What failed?
  > 
  > What did we learn?
  > 
  > What must improve?
  > 
  > How do we prevent recurrence?
  > Executive Postmortem Structure
  > Section 1
  > 
  > Executive Summary
  > 
  > Section 2
  > 
  > Business Impact
  > 
  > Section 3
  > 
  > Incident Timeline
  > 
  > Section 4
  > 
  > Root Cause Summary
  > 
  > Section 5
  > 
  > What Went Well
  > 
  > Section 6
  > 
  > What Went Poorly
  > 
  > Section 7
  > 
  > Corrective Actions
  > 
  > Section 8
  > 
  > Preventive Actions
  > 
  > Section 9
  > 
  > Lessons Learned
  > 
  > Section 10
  > 
  > Governance Improvements
  > 
  > Example — Hallucination Incident
  > Executive Summary
  > AI Assistant generated
  > incorrect insurance recommendations
  > for 127 users.
  > Root Cause
  > Retrieval Quality Failure
  > What Went Well
  > Monitoring detected issue
  > 
  > Alerting worked
  > 
  > Response SLA met
  > What Went Poorly
  > No citation validation
  > 
  > No fallback retrieval
  > Lessons Learned
  > Groundedness monitoring required
  > 
  > Retrieval testing required
  > Governance Improvement
  > Mandatory RAG evaluation
  > before production deployment
  > Example — Prompt Injection Incident
  > Root Cause
  > Input Validation Missing
  > What Went Well
  > Security team responded quickly
  > What Went Poorly
  > Prompt firewall absent
  > Lessons Learned
  > Prompt security testing
  > must be mandatory
  > Example — Agent Incident
  > Problem
  > Agent approved claims
  > without human review
  > Root Cause
  > HITL control missing
  > Governance Improvement
  > Approval workflows required
  > for autonomous decisions
  > Example — MCP Incident
  > Problem
  > Unauthorized database deletion
  > Root Cause
  > Shared credentials
  > Lessons Learned
  > RBAC enforcement mandatory
  > What Went Well Section
  > 
  > Capture:
  > 
  > Detection
  > 
  > Response
  > 
  > Communication
  > 
  > Escalation
  > 
  > Recovery
  > 
  > Example
  > 
  > Alert generated within 30 seconds
  > 
  > Response team engaged in 10 minutes
  > 
  > Service restored in 45 minutes
  > What Went Poorly Section
  > 
  > Capture:
  > 
  > Failures
  > 
  > Delays
  > 
  > Missed Controls
  > 
  > Process Weaknesses
  > 
  > Example
  > 
  > No rollback plan
  > 
  > No fallback model
  > 
  > Missing approvals
  > Lessons Learned Categories
  > Category	Example
  > Governance	Missing Policies
  > Security	Weak Controls
  > AI	Poor Evaluation
  > AgentOps	Missing Safeguards
  > Data	Data Quality Issues
  > Process	Change Management Gap
  > Operations	Monitoring Gap
  > Compliance	Policy Violations
  > Governance Improvement Categories
  > Policy Improvements
  > New AI Policy
  > Control Improvements
  > New Security Controls
  > Monitoring Improvements
  > Additional KRIs
  > Training Improvements
  > Awareness Programs
  > Architecture Improvements
  > Fallback Systems
  > Postmortem Lifecycle
  > Incident Closed
  >       ↓
  > RCA Completed
  >       ↓
  > Postmortem Created
  >       ↓
  > Review Meeting
  >       ↓
  > Actions Approved
  >       ↓
  > Knowledge Shared
  > Runtime Flow
  > Incident
  >       ↓
  > Timeline
  >       ↓
  > RCA
  >       ↓
  > Postmortem
  >       ↓
  > Knowledge Base
  >       ↓
  > Governance Updates
  > Blameless Postmortem Principle
  > 
  > Bad
  > 
  > Who Made The Mistake?
  > 
  > Good
  > 
  > What Allowed The Mistake?
  > 
  > Focus
  > 
  > Systems
  > 
  > Processes
  > 
  > Controls
  > 
  > Governance
  > 
  > Not individuals.
  > 
  > Production Issues
  > 1. Blame Culture
  > 
  > Problem
  > 
  > People Hide Problems
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Blameless Reviews
  > 2. No Executive Summary
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Business Impact Focus
  > 3. Lessons Never Shared
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Knowledge Repository
  > 4. Actions Never Implemented
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Action Tracking
  > 5. AI Incidents Not Documented Properly
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > AI-Specific Templates
  > 6. Governance Weakness Ignored
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Control Review
  > 7. Repeat Incidents
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Recurrence Analysis
  > 8. Executive Decisions Missing
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Decision Logging
  > 9. No Knowledge Transfer
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Training Updates
  > 10. Postmortem Becomes Archive
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Continuous Improvement Tracking

  > Future Prevention
  > Brutal Reality
  > 
  > Most organizations document incidents.
  > 
  > Few organizations learn from incidents.
  > 
  > Elite organizations turn every incident into:
  > 
  > Better Governance
  > 
  > Better Controls
  > 
  > Better Architecture
  > 
  > Better Processes
  > 
  > Better Teams
  > 
  > A postmortem is not about documenting failure.
  > 97. incident_rca — Enterprise Root Cause Analysis (RCA), Failure Investigation & Recurrence Prevention Framework
  > 
  > An incident is not truly resolved until the organization understands:
  > 
  > Why it happened
  > 
  > What failed
  > 
  > What allowed it to happen
  > 
  > How to prevent it from happening again
  > 
  > That is the purpose of:
  > 
  > incident_rca
  > Why RCA Exists
  > 
  > Bad Organizations
  > 
  > Fix Symptoms
  > 
  > Example
  > 
  > Restart Service
  > 
  > Problem returns.
  > 
  > Mature Organizations
  > 
  > Fix Root Cause
  > 
  > Problem prevented permanently.
  > 
  > Governance Hierarchy
  > Incident
  >       ↓
  > Timeline
  >       ↓
  > Investigation
  >       ↓
  > Root Cause Analysis
  >       ↓
  > Remediation
  >       ↓
  > Prevention
  > Purpose
  > Area	Purpose
  > Main Purpose	Root Cause Identification
  > Governance	Prevent Recurrence
  > Security	Breach Investigation
  > Operations	Failure Analysis
  > Compliance	Corrective Action
  > Executive	Organizational Learning
  > Relationship
  > incident_management
  >         ↓
  > incident_rca
  >         ↓
  > remediation_plan
  > 
  > Connected To
  > 
  > incident_management
  > 
  > incident_timeline
  > 
  > audit_finding
  > 
  > risk_register
  > 
  > remediation_plan
  > 
  > control_testing
  > 
  > lessons_learned
  > SQL Table
  > CREATE TABLE incident_rca (
  > 
  >     rca_id VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
  > 
  >     incident_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     rca_title VARCHAR(500),
  > 
  >     investigation_owner VARCHAR(200),
  > 
  >     primary_root_cause TEXT,
  > 
  >     contributing_factors TEXT,
  > 
  >     failed_controls TEXT,
  > 
  >     impacted_processes TEXT,
  > 
  >     corrective_actions TEXT,
  > 
  >     preventive_actions TEXT,
  > 
  >     recurrence_probability VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     executive_summary TEXT,
  > 
  >     approval_status VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     completed_at TIMESTAMP,
  > 
  >     created_at TIMESTAMP
  > );
  > RCA Questions
  > 
  > Every RCA should answer:
  > 
  > What happened?
  > 
  > Why did it happen?
  > 
  > Why was it not detected earlier?
  > 
  > Which controls failed?
  > 
  > Could it happen again?
  > 
  > What must change?
  > RCA Techniques
  > Method	Purpose
  > 5 Whys	Deep Cause Discovery
  > Fishbone	Cause Categorization
  > Fault Tree Analysis	Failure Paths
  > Event Analysis	Timeline Investigation
  > Change Analysis	What Changed
  > Barrier Analysis	Failed Controls
  > Causal Mapping	Multi-Cause Analysis
  > Technique 1 — 5 Whys
  > 
  > Incident
  > 
  > Hallucination Caused Wrong Recommendation
  > 
  > Why #1
  > 
  > Model Produced Wrong Answer
  > 
  > Why?
  > 
  > Poor Retrieval
  > 
  > Why #2
  > 
  > Poor Retrieval
  > 
  > Why?
  > 
  > Chunking Incorrect
  > 
  > Why #3
  > 
  > Chunking Incorrect
  > 
  > Why?
  > 
  > Document Parser Failed
  > 
  > Why #4
  > 
  > Parser Failed
  > 
  > Why?
  > 
  > Schema Changed
  > 
  > Why #5
  > 
  > Schema Changed
  > 
  > Why?
  > 
  > No Change Management Process
  > 
  > Root Cause
  > 
  > Missing Change Governance
  > Technique 2 — Fishbone Analysis
  > 
  > Categories
  > 
  > People
  > 
  > Process
  > 
  > Technology
  > 
  > Data
  > 
  > Governance
  > 
  > Environment
  > 
  > Example
  > 
  > Prompt Injection Incident
  > 
  > People
  > 
  > Security Training Gap
  > 
  > Process
  > 
  > No Prompt Review
  > 
  > Technology
  > 
  > Weak Guardrails
  > 
  > Data
  > 
  > Sensitive Context Exposed
  > 
  > Governance
  > 
  > Policy Missing
  > Technique 3 — Fault Tree
  > PII Exposure
  >        ↓
  > Prompt Injection
  >        ↓
  > Guardrail Failure
  >        ↓
  > Input Validation Failure
  > 
  > Root Cause Found.
  > 
  > AI Incident RCA Examples
  > Hallucination Incident
  > 
  > Symptom
  > 
  > Incorrect Answer
  > 
  > Root Cause
  > 
  > Retrieval Quality Failure
  > 
  > Contributing Factors
  > 
  > Poor Chunking
  > 
  > Weak Embeddings
  > 
  > No Reranker
  > 
  > Corrective Actions
  > 
  > Hybrid Search
  > 
  > Reranking
  > 
  > Evaluation Framework
  > Prompt Injection Incident
  > 
  > Root Cause
  > 
  > Input Validation Missing
  > 
  > Contributing Factors
  > 
  > Weak Prompt Shield
  > 
  > No Content Filtering
  > 
  > Prevention
  > 
  > Prompt Firewall
  > 
  > Security Testing
  > Agent Incident
  > 
  > Problem
  > 
  > Agent Approved Claims
  > Without Review
  > 
  > Root Cause
  > 
  > HITL Workflow Missing
  > 
  > Contributing Factors
  > 
  > Poor Governance
  > 
  > Weak Testing
  > MCP Incident
  > 
  > Problem
  > 
  > Unauthorized Database Delete
  > 
  > Root Cause
  > 
  > Shared Credentials
  > 
  > Prevention
  > 
  > RBAC
  > 
  > Credential Rotation
  > Model Drift Incident
  > 
  > Problem
  > 
  > Accuracy Drop
  > 
  > Root Cause
  > 
  > Data Distribution Shift
  > 
  > Prevention
  > 
  > Drift Monitoring
  > RCA Structure
  > Section 1
  > 
  > Incident Summary

  > Section 2

  > Production Issues
  > 1. Blame-Based RCA
  > 
  > Bad
  > 
  > Developer Mistake
  > 
  > Good
  > 
  > Process Allowed Failure
  > 2. Symptom Treated As Root Cause
  > 
  > Example
  > 
  > Server Restarted
  > 
  > Not root cause.
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Underlying Failure
  > 3. Missing Contributing Factors
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Full Cause Chain
  > 4. No Failed Control Analysis
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Control Effectiveness Review
  > 5. AI Incidents Misdiagnosed
  > 
  > Actual Cause
  > 
  > Retrieval Failure
  > 
  > Reported As
  > 
  > LLM Failure
  > 
  > Wrong.
  > 
  > 6. No Preventive Actions
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Future Controls
  > 7. Executive Summary Missing
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Business Impact Summary
  > 8. No Approval Process
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Governance Review
  > 9. Lessons Not Shared
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Knowledge Management
  > 10. Recurrence Tracking Missing
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Repeat Incident Analysis

  > Governance Improvement
  > Brutal Reality
  > 
  > Many organizations close incidents when:
  > 
  > Systems Are Running Again
  > 
  > Mature organizations close incidents only when:
  > 
  > Root Cause Is Known
  > 
  > Controls Are Improved
  > 
  > Lessons Are Captured
  > 
  > Recurrence Is Prevented
  > 
  > The purpose of RCA is not to explain the past.
  > 
  > The purpose of RCA is to prevent the future.
  > 
  > Next: 98. incident_postmortem
  > 
  > This becomes the formal executive and organizational learning document covering:
  > 
  > Executive Summary
  > Business Impact
  > Timeline Review
  > Root Cause Summary
  > What Went Well
  > What Went Poorly
  > Corrective Actions
  > Preventive Actions
  > Lessons Learned
  > Process Improvements
  > Governance Improvements
  > 96. incident_timeline — Enterprise Incident Forensics, Chronology & Audit Reconstruction Framework
  > 
  > This is the single source of truth for everything that happened during an incident.
  > 
  > When executives, auditors, regulators, legal teams, security teams, or investigators ask:
  > 
  > What happened?
  > 
  > When did it happen?
  > 
  > Who responded?
  > 
  > What actions were taken?
  > 
  > Were SLAs met?
  > 
  > Who approved decisions?
  > 
  > The answer comes from:
  > 
  > incident_timeline
  > Why Incident Timeline Exists
  > 
  > Incident Report says:
  > 
  > Prompt Injection Incident
  > 
  > Question:
  > 
  > How did the event unfold?
  > 
  > Need complete chronology.
  > 
  > Governance Hierarchy
  > Incident
  >     ↓
  > Timeline
  >     ↓
  > Investigation
  >     ↓
  > RCA
  >     ↓
  > Audit
  > Purpose
  > Area	Purpose
  > Main Purpose	Event Chronology
  > Governance	Accountability
  > Security	Forensics
  > Audit	Evidence
  > Compliance	Regulatory Review
  > Legal	Chain of Events
  > Relationship
  > incident_management
  >        ↓
  > incident_timeline
  >        ↓
  > incident_rca
  > 
  > Connected To
  > 
  > incident_management
  > 
  > risk_alert
  > 
  > risk_escalation
  > 
  > audit_log
  > 
  > security_event
  > 
  > incident_war_room
  > 
  > incident_communication
  > SQL Table
  > CREATE TABLE incident_timeline (
  > 
  >     timeline_id VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
  > 
  >     incident_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     event_timestamp TIMESTAMP,
  > 
  >     event_type VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     event_category VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     event_description TEXT,
  > 
  >     actor_type VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     actor_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     related_object_type VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     related_object_id VARCHAR(100),
  > 
  >     severity VARCHAR(50),
  > 
  >     evidence_reference TEXT,
  > 
  >     created_at TIMESTAMP
  > );
  > Timeline Event Categories
  > Category	Example
  > Detection	Alert Triggered
  > Escalation	Manager Notified
  > Response	Team Assigned
  > Containment	Agent Disabled
  > Investigation	RCA Started
  > Communication	Executive Update
  > Security	Credential Revoked
  > Compliance	Legal Notified
  > Recovery	Service Restored
  > Closure	Incident Closed

  > Timeline SLA Analysis
  > 
  > Example
  > 
  > Alert
  > 
  > 09:00
  > 
  > Assignment
  > 
  > 09:05
  > 
  > Response Time
  > 
  > Response Time=09:05−09:00=5 Minutes

  > SLA Met.
  > 
  > Runtime Flow
  > Incident Event
  >       ↓
  > Timeline Entry

  > Audit Trail
  >       ↓
  > Investigation
  > Production Issues
  > 1. Missing Timeline Events
  > 
  > Investigation asks:
  > 
  > What Happened?
  > 
  > Nobody knows.
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Automatic Event Logging
  > 2. Manual Updates
  > 
  > Events entered later.
  > 
  > Timeline inaccurate.
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Real-Time Capture
  > 3. Missing Actor Information
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > User/Agent Attribution
  > 4. Missing Executive Actions
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Decision Logging
  > 5. Missing Regulatory Events
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Compliance Timeline
  > 6. AI Events Not Logged
  > 
  > Traditional systems log:
  > 
  > Server Failure
  > 
  > Not
  > 
  > Hallucination
  > 
  > Agent Loop
  > 
  > Prompt Injection
  > 7. Timeline Manipulation
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Immutable Records
  > 8. Multi-System Events Missing
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Cross-System Correlation
  > 9. No Time Synchronization
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > NTP Standardization
  > 10. No Evidence Links
  > 
  > Need
  > 
  > Evidence References

  > incident_timeline_regulatory	Regulatory Events
  > incident_timeline_correlation	Cross-System Tracing
  > incident_timeline_attachment	Supporting Files
  > incident_timeline_audit	Audit Trail
  > incident_timeline_dashboard	Reporting
  > Production-Grade AI Timeline Events
  > Event	Example
  > Hallucination Event	Wrong Answer Generated
  > Prompt Injection Event	Security Bypass Attempt
  > Agent Loop Event	Iteration Limit Exceeded
  > MCP Event	Unauthorized Tool Call
  > Drift Event	Accuracy Drop
  > HITL Event	Approval Bypass
  > Cost Event	Budget Spike
  > Explainability Event	Missing Reasoning
  > Memory Event	Sensitive Data Exposure
  > Multi-Agent Event	Workflow Cascade Failure
  > Enterprise AI Forensics Architecture
  > Risk Alert
  >       ↓
  > Incident
  >       ↓
  > Timeline
  >       ↓
  > Evidence
  >       ↓
  > RCA
  >       ↓
  > Audit
  >       ↓
  > Legal Review
  > Brutal Reality
  > 
  > During an audit, lawsuit, regulatory review, or postmortem, memory is worthless.
  > 
  > Only evidence matters.
  > 
  > The incident timeline becomes the authoritative historical record that proves:
  > 
  > What happened
  > 
  > When it happened
  > 
  > Who acted
  > 
  > Why decisions were made

  > 94. risk_escalation — Enterprise Risk Escalation, Accountability & Crisis Governance Framework
  > 
  > This table answers a very important question:
  > 
  > A risk has been detected.
  > 
  > Who must act?
  > 
  > How quickly?
  > 
  > Who gets notified next?
  > 
  > When does leadership become involved?
  > 
  > When do regulators get notified?
  > 
  > This is the formal accountability chain of Enterprise AI Governance.
  > 
  > Why Risk Escalation Exists
  > 
  > Alert
  > 
  > Prompt Injection Detected
  > 
  > Question
  > 
  > Who owns it?
  > 
  > Security Team?
  > 
  > Manager?
  > 
  > CISO?
  > 
  > Board?
  > 
  > Need:
  > 
  > Escalation Path
  > 
  > Ownership
  > 
  > SLA
  > 
  > Decision Authority
  > Governance Hierarchy
  > Risk
  >     ↓
  > KRI
  >     ↓
  > Alert
  >     ↓
  > Escalation
  >     ↓
  > Incident
  >     ↓
  > Executive Action

  > Main Purpose	Accountability
  > Governance	Decision Escalation
  > Security	Crisis Management

  > Operations	Response Management
  > Executive	Leadership Visibility
  > Relationship
  > risk_alert
  >       ↓
  > risk_escalation

  > 
  >     created_at TIMESTAMP

  > 93. risk_alert_rule — Enterprise Risk Detection, Correlation & Escalation Decision Engine
  > 
  > This is the brain behind Enterprise Risk Monitoring.
  > 
  > Without this table:
  > 
  > KRIs exist
  > 
  > Alerts exist
  > 
  > But nobody knows
  > WHEN an alert should fire
  > 
  > This table defines the logic.
  > 
  > Why Risk Alert Rules Exist
  > 
  > KRI
  > 
  > Hallucination Rate = 4.8%
  > 
  > Question
  > 
  > Should we trigger an alert?
  > 
  > Depends on:
  > 
  > Threshold
  > 
  > Trend
  > 
  > Velocity
  > 
  > Business Context
  > 
  > Risk Appetite
  > Governance Hierarchy
  > Risk
  >     ↓
  > KRI

## 2026-06-10T23:34:27.755151+00:00 · bcc5d9a3
- **Source**: claude_code
- **Category**: user
- **Session**: 2026-06-10
- **Prompt**:

  > Final Multi-Agent Project Checklist
  > 1. Terminal Must Show
  > Area	Must Show
  > Request	request ID, user, tenant, session
  > Input	raw input, cleaned input, files, metadata
  > Plan	task breakdown, sequence, dependency
  > Agent Flow	current agent, next agent, previous agent
  > Supervisor	routing, approval, escalation decision
  > State	queued, running, waiting, failed, completed
  > Tool/MCP	tool name, payload, response, latency
  > RAG	query rewrite, top-k docs, score, citations
  > Memory	memory read/write, context used
  > Review	confidence, risk, quality score
  > Error	error type, stack trace, retry count
  > Testing	test case, expected, actual, pass/fail
  > Cost	tokens, model cost, tool cost
  > Trace	trace ID across all components
  > 2. UI Must Show
  > UI Section	Purpose
  > Input Panel	user query, upload, workflow selection
  > Plan View	agent-generated plan
  > Process Timeline	step-by-step execution
  > Agent Map	planner → worker → reviewer → supervisor
  > Status Board	running, waiting, blocked, failed
  > Output Panel	final result
  > Review Panel	validation, confidence, citations
  > Human Approval	approve, reject, edit, send back
  > Error Panel	simple error + fix suggestion
  > Testing Panel	eval score, failed cases
  > Registry UI	agents, skills, tools, MCP, prompts
  > Audit Trail	who did what and when
  > Cost Dashboard	token/API/workflow cost
  > Incident Dashboard	live failures and SLA breach
  > 3. Agents Needed
  > Agent	Role
  > Supervisor Agent	controls full workflow
  > Planner Agent	breaks task into steps
  > Router Agent	selects agent/tool/model
  > Worker Agent	performs main task
  > Retriever Agent	RAG/search/vector retrieval
  > Tool Agent	API/MCP/tool execution
  > Memory Agent	stores/retrieves context
  > Reflection Agent	self-checks answer
  > Reviewer Agent	validates final output
  > Security Agent	PII/RBAC/prompt injection
  > Testing Agent	runs test/evaluation
  > Inflection/Escalation Agent	detects failure/human-needed case
  > Cost Agent	controls token/API cost
  > Monitoring Agent	health, logs, metrics
  > Compliance Agent	audit, retention, policy
  > 4. Registries Needed
  > Registry	Stores
  > Agent Registry	agent name, role, owner, version
  > Skill Registry	reusable skills
  > Tool Registry	APIs/tools
  > MCP Registry	MCP servers
  > Prompt Registry	prompt versions
  > Workflow Registry	workflow steps
  > Policy Registry	rules/guardrails
  > Evaluation Registry	tests/benchmarks
  > Model Registry	model name, version, provider
  > Dataset Registry	source docs/index version
  > Approval Registry	approval rules/history
  > Incident Registry	failures/root cause
  > Cost Registry	token/tool/user cost
  > Access Registry	role/agent permissions
  > 5. Production Controls Missing in Many Demos
  > Control	Required
  > State Machine	every step has lifecycle
  > Checkpoint/Resume	restart from last good step
  > Retry Policy	retry count + backoff
  > Timeout Policy	stop hanging agents/tools
  > Dead Letter Queue	store failed tasks
  > Fallback Model	GPT → Claude → Gemini → local
  > Fallback Tool	backup API/manual queue
  > Kill Switch	disable agent/tool/workflow
  > Rollback	prompt/model/workflow rollback
  > Rate Limit	per user/team/API
  > Token Budget	max tokens per request
  > Cost Limit	daily/monthly budget
  > Concurrency Control	avoid state overwrite
  > Queue/Event Bus	async workflow handling
  > Secrets Manager	no API key in logs
  > 6. Security Must Have
  > Area	Requirement
  > RBAC	role-based access
  > ABAC	department/location/context access
  > Tenant Isolation	one client cannot see another
  > Document Security	file-level permission check
  > PII Redaction	mask sensitive data
  > Prompt Injection Defense	block malicious doc/user prompt
  > Output Guardrail	final answer policy check
  > Tool Permission	which agent can call which tool
  > Human Approval	for risky actions
  > Audit Logs	immutable logs
  > 7. Testing Must Have
  > Test Type	Purpose
  > Unit Test	test each skill/tool
  > Agent Test	test each agent behavior
  > Workflow Test	test full flow
  > RAG Test	retrieval quality
  > Regression Test	old use cases still pass
  > Security Test	injection/PII/access
  > Load Test	many users
  > Latency Test	response time
  > Cost Test	token/API cost
  > Human Review Test	approval flow
  > Golden Test Set	standard expected answers
  > Synthetic Data Test	safe fake data
  > 8. Observability Must Have
  > Metric	Track
  > Trace ID	end-to-end tracking
  > Agent Latency	time per agent
  > Tool Latency	API/MCP time
  > Model Latency	LLM response time
  > Token Usage	prompt/completion tokens
  > Cost	per request/user/team
  > Error Rate	failed workflows
  > Retry Count	unstable components
  > Hallucination Risk	output quality
  > Retrieval Score	RAG quality
  > Approval Rate	human-in-loop usage
  > SLA/SLO	uptime/performance
  > 9. Final Architecture Flow
  > User Input
  >    ↓
  > Auth / RBAC / Session
  >    ↓
  > Input Validation / PII Check
  >    ↓
  > Supervisor Agent
  >    ↓
  > Planner Agent
  >    ↓
  > Router Agent
  >    ↓
  > Agent Registry / Skill Registry / Tool Registry / MCP Registry
  >    ↓
  > Worker Agents + Tool Agents + Retriever Agent
  >    ↓
  > Memory + RAG + API/MCP Calls
  >    ↓
  > Reflection Agent
  >    ↓
  > Reviewer Agent
  >    ↓
  > Security / Compliance Check
  >    ↓
  > Human Approval if Needed
  >    ↓
  > Final Output
  >    ↓
  > Audit Log + Metrics + Feedback
  >    ↓
  > Testing / Evaluation / Learning Loop
  > Brutal Final Truth
  > 
  > Minimum production-ready system needs:
  > 
  > Agent orchestration + registry + state machine + retry + fallback + security + testing + observability + human approval + audit + cost control.
